    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '75b02423-c64b-48be-abf7-dba60b2284e1'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d7907180" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>Nice wrap</b>: Graphene oxide (GO) wrapped around disodium terephthalate (Na<sub>2</sub>TP) is prepared through an anti-solvent precipitation method (see figure). GO is reduced during the first several cycles of charge/discharge leading to an outstanding cyclic performance of the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4b80bce7-c81e-44cb-ad3a-f27d489f1770/chem201703418-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4b80bce7-c81e-44cb-ad3a-f27d489f1770/chem201703418-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f2ad636d-4f13-4ca9-9817-d49424f48109/chem201703418-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4b80bce7-c81e-44cb-ad3a-f27d489f1770/chem201703418-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d7907183" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>As a promising candidate for large-scale energy storage systems, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are experiencing a rapid development. Organic conjugated carboxylic acid anodes not only have tailorable electrochemical properties but also are easily accessible. However, the low stability and electrical conductivity hamper their practical applications. In this study, disodium terephthalate (Na<sub>2</sub>TP), the most favorable organic conjugated carboxylic acid anode material for SIBs, was proposed to integrate with graphene oxide (GO) by an anti-solvent precipitation process, which ensures the uniform and tight coating of GO on the Na<sub>2</sub>TP surface. GO is electrochemically reduced during the first several cycles of the electrochemical measurement, which buffers the volume change and improves the electrical conductivity of Na<sub>2</sub>TP, resulting in a better cyclic and rate performance. The incorporation of only 5 wt % GO onto Na<sub>2</sub>TP leads to a reversible capability of 235 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at a current rate of 0.1 C, which is the best among the state of the art organic anodes for SIBs. The one-step synthesis together with the low costs of the raw materials show a promise for the scalable preparation of anode materials for practical SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="chem201703418-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="chem201703418-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The emerging demand of renewable energy and smart grid technology stimulated the development of large-scale energy storage systems with low costs.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0001_R_d7907172e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#chem201703418-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0003_R_d7907172e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a></span> Compared to the widely used lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have the advantages of low costs, a high natural abundance, and a low environmental impact, which make them more appropriate for the above applications.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0004_R_d7907172e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nature">4</a>-<a href="#chem201703418-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0007_R_d7907172e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">7</a></span> Although massive efforts have been made on the development of SIBs, the search for anode materials with appropriately low redox potentials, large specific capacities, and high structure stabilities remains a challenge for their practical use.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0008_R_d7907172e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#chem201703418-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0012_R_d7907172e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Storage Mater.">12</a></span> Due to their flexible structures and the easily tailored redox properties, organic electrode materials attract great interests in recent years.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0013_R_d7907172e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a>-<a href="#chem201703418-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0024_R_d7907172e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">24</a></span> In contrast to inorganic materials, organic materials are inexpensive, which can originate from renewable resources,<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0025_R_d7907172e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Commun.">25</a></span> and meanwhile, they can be easily disposed through low-temperature combustion.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0026_R_d7907172e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">26</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Organic conjugated carboxylic acids represent one of the most promising organic electrode materials towards practical application,<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0019_R_d7907172e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a>, <a href="#chem201703418-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0027_R_d7907172e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">27</a>-<a href="#chem201703418-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0029_R_d7907172e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Mater. Chem. A">29</a></span> which store sodium through a reversible two-electron redox reaction with a low potential resulting from the loss of resonance or aromaticity. Terephthalic acid (TPA) and its derivatives are the mostly studied organic conjugated carboxylic acids because of their easy accessibility and superior sodium storage capability.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0015_R_d7907172e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a>-<a href="#chem201703418-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0017_R_d7907172e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Environ. Sci.">17</a>, <a href="#chem201703418-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0030_R_d7907172e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Power Sources">30</a></span> For example, disodium terephthalate (Na<sub>2</sub>TP) has a high theoretical capacity of 255 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and a redox potential of approximately 0.4 V versus the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na couple, which is high enough to prevent the formation of sodium dendrite and low enough to sustain a high energy density for a full cell.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0016_R_d7907172e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></span> Tachibana et al. proposed a preparation routine of TPA from biomass originated furfural,<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0031_R_d7907172e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Sci. Rep.">31</a></span> and Zhao et al. firstly utilized Na<sub>2</sub>TP as an anode material of a SIB, thereby achieving a reversible specific capacity of about 270 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> in the first cycle at a current rate of 0.1 C.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0015_R_d7907172e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></span> The significant drawbacks of Na<sub>2</sub>TP are its pulverization during charge/discharge and its low electrical conductivity.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0014_R_d7907172e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a></span> In order to improve the performance of Na<sub>2</sub>TP, Wan et al. synthesized Na<sub>2</sub>TP nanosheets and the transport distance of the Na<sup>+</sup> ions and the electrons was effectively shortened, but it still had a low electrical conductivity.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0032_R_d7907172e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nano Energy">32</a></span> The electrochemical performance of organic conjugated carboxylic acids can be enhanced through the incorporation of conductive graphene,<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0027_R_d7907172e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">27</a>, <a href="#chem201703418-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0033_R_d7907172e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Power Sources">33</a>-<a href="#chem201703418-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0036_R_d7907172e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Bull. Korean Chem. Soc.">36</a></span> in which their structure stability and conductivity can be effectively enhanced. However, the preparation methods of graphene/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid materials reported so far are fairly complicated and time consuming, which usually includes ultrasonication, spray drying, or heat treatment.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we report a one-step anti-solvent precipitation method, which is commonly used in pharmaceutical processes, to prepare a graphene oxide (GO)/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid. GO wraps on the surface of the Na<sub>2</sub>TP particles and is reduced, forming the graphene/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid during the activation process of the SIB with this hybrid as anode. The graphene helps to decrease the charge-transfer resistance of the Na<sub>2</sub>TP, and as a result, a much better cyclic stability is achieved only with a small content of GO (≈5 wt %, denoted 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP) compared with pure Na<sub>2</sub>TP. 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP still delivers a capacity of 235 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, which is the best cyclic performance among the state of the art organic anodes for SIBs. Together with its appropriate redox potential and the low cost of the raw materials, the electrochemically reduced GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid reveals to be a promising anode for environmentally friendly SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="chem201703418-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="chem201703418-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid were synthesized through the anti-solvent precipitation method, which is schematically shown in Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0001">1</a> a. Briefly, a suspension of GO and Na<sub>2</sub>TP was mixed with superfluous ethanol leading to the precipitation of the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid. The as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>TP particles were micro-sized particles as shown in Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0001">1</a> b as well as in Figures S1 a and b in the Supporting Information, and for the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid, GO can be clearly observed, which wraps on the surface of the Na<sub>2</sub>TP particles (Figures <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0001">1</a> c and Figure S1 c–h in the Supporting Information). It is shown that the incorporation of GO does not change the morphology of the Na<sub>2</sub>TP particles, and most of the Na<sub>2</sub>TP particles are uncovered with a low GO ratio (1 wt %) whereas the aggregation of GO appears with a ratio higher than 10 wt % (Figures S1 c–h in the Supporting Information). In the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (Figures <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0001">1</a> d and S2 in the Supporting Information), the characteristic peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>TP are well maintained in the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid, indicating that GO does not affect the crystal structure of Na<sub>2</sub>TP.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0016_R_d7907172e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></span> The Raman spectrum of 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP (Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0001">1</a> f) exhibits a featured D-band at approximately <img alt="urn:x-wiley:09476539:media:chem201703418:chem201703418-math-0001" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/1cb2258a-0fa9-4629-936c-e1371fd644b0/chem201703418-math-0001.png">
=1349 cm<sup>−1</sup> and G-band at about <img alt="urn:x-wiley:09476539:media:chem201703418:chem201703418-math-0002" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/85b50dbe-4e65-42a8-8dd7-14829489ec19/chem201703418-math-0002.png">
=1591 cm<sup>−1</sup> of GO, whereas the characteristic bands of Na<sub>2</sub>TP are still visible, which is in accordance with the XRD results.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0037_R_d7907172e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Carbon">37</a>, <a href="#chem201703418-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0038_R_d7907172e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">38</a></span> No distinguished difference is observed between the Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectra of Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid (Figures <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0001">1</a> e and S3 in the Supporting Information), and the typical metal carboxylate bands at <img alt="urn:x-wiley:09476539:media:chem201703418:chem201703418-math-0003" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/9efea3d0-c662-4405-8a88-a8ecc409ed34/chem201703418-math-0003.png">
=1551 and 1380 cm<sup>−1</sup> are assigned to the ν<sub>as</sub>(COONa) and ν<sub>s</sub>(COONa) modes, respectively.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0016_R_d7907172e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#chem201703418-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0017_R_d7907172e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Environ. Sci.">17</a></span> The typical peaks for the functional groups of GO cannot be observed, which is possibly due to its very low content.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="chem201703418-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dae07cf0-5944-4a58-b3d9-c355849a97ce/chem201703418-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/dae07cf0-5944-4a58-b3d9-c355849a97ce/chem201703418-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9d99ae55-8b5a-4541-b6e3-b63b2c9843d8/chem201703418-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dae07cf0-5944-4a58-b3d9-c355849a97ce/chem201703418-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201703418-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201703418" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Schematic diagram of the one-step GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid synthesis process. b,c) Scanning electron microscope (SEM) images of the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid, respectively. d) XRD patterns, e) FTIR spectra, and f) Raman spectra of Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The thermogravimetric (TG) analysis of Na<sub>2</sub>TP (Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0002">2</a> a) only shows a dissociation peak at 655.8 °C implying that pure Na<sub>2</sub>TP was synthesized,<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0016_R_d7907172e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></span> whereas the analysis of the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid shows a dissociation peak at 644.4 °C, which is 11.4 °C lower than that of Na<sub>2</sub>TP, which may result from the decomposition of GO at lower temperature. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption isotherms of Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid (Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0002">2</a> b) show a type II character, suggesting no micro- or mesopores exist, and the specific surface area (SSA) of Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid was 2.64 and 4.12 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The slightly increase of the SSA may be contributed by GO in the hybrid.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="chem201703418-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/230b1028-9957-49f0-baaf-d6c5f5a32025/chem201703418-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/230b1028-9957-49f0-baaf-d6c5f5a32025/chem201703418-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fd578a96-dd85-453d-a7b4-f3c246b6269f/chem201703418-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/230b1028-9957-49f0-baaf-d6c5f5a32025/chem201703418-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201703418-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201703418" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) TG profiles of Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid. b) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption isotherms of Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of GO was preliminary studied by using a CR2032-type coin cell with a sodium foil as counter electrode. Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0003">3</a> a shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of GO in the potential range 0.001–2.5 V versus the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na couple at a scan rate of 2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The broad reduction peak in the range 1.0–1.7 V is ascribed to the electrochemical reduction of GO.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0039_R_d7907172e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Mater.">39</a>, <a href="#chem201703418-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0040_R_d7907172e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Mater. Chem.">40</a></span> The reduction degree of GO can be monitored by the change of the current of this reduction peak. The electrochemical reduction of GO is completed when the reduction peak disappears; it is an irreversible process and the reduction peak decays very quickly after the second cycle.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0039_R_d7907172e684" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Mater.">39</a></span> Meanwhile, the CV curves of Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid were investigated. In the first cycle, Na<sub>2</sub>TP reveals a cathode peak at approximately 0.75 V corresponding to the irreversible decomposition of the electrolyte on the electrode surface to form a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and a peak at about 0.1 V correlated to the sodium storage in the conductive additive, that is, Super P (Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0003">3</a> b). The 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid does not reveal the SEI formation peak around about 0.75 V, which changes to a very broad peak in the potential range 0.5–0.8 V in the first cycle (Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0005">5</a> c), suggesting the Na<sub>2</sub>TP surface is wrapped by GO. Both Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid reveal discharge peaks at approximately 0.3 V and charge peaks at about 0.5 V. The peak at around 0.3 V is attributed to the reduction of the two carbonyl groups of Na<sub>2</sub>TP for the insertion of two Na<sup>+</sup> ions, whereas the peak at approximately 0.5 V is attributed to the desertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. However, the reduction peak of GO is not visible in the CV curves of the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid because of the low content of GO and the relatively low current density.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="chem201703418-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/38f0641c-ca7c-4d5f-99ae-2f9c480f05fc/chem201703418-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/38f0641c-ca7c-4d5f-99ae-2f9c480f05fc/chem201703418-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6613a0ab-0423-497d-95cd-bbc164d23ac9/chem201703418-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/38f0641c-ca7c-4d5f-99ae-2f9c480f05fc/chem201703418-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201703418-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201703418" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>CV curves of a) GO, b) Na<sub>2</sub>TP, and c) the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid at a scan rate of 2 mV s<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range 0.001–2.5 V versus the Na<sup>+</sup>/Na couple.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The sodium storage performance of Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid was tested by using a sodium foil as counter electrode at a current density of 30 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (0.1 C) in the voltage range 0.001–2.5 V. The discharge profile of the first cycle of Na<sub>2</sub>TP displays three plateaus located at approximately 0.7, 0.3, and 0.1 V, which agrees well with the CV curves (Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0004">4</a> a). The prominent plateau at about 0.7 V indicates the large amount of irreversible reactions to form a SEI, which leads to a low initial coulombic efficiency (CE) of 51 %. The plateau at approximately 0.3 and 0.1 V correspond to sodium storage in Na<sub>2</sub>TP and Super P, respectively. The reversible capacity of the first cycle is 367 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. Because the theoretical capacity of Na<sub>2</sub>TP is 255 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, the increased capacity of 112 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> originates from the reversible storage of sodium in Super P. Super P can at most contribute 148 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> to the capacity based on its weight ratio and the galvanostatic charge/discharge results shown in Figure S4 in the Supporting Information. Compared with Na<sub>2</sub>TP, the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid delivers a reversible capacity of 354 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> in the first cycle. The capacity of the plateau at approximately 0.7 V increases remarkably with the incorporation of GO, indicating an increasing of the irreversible capacity (Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0004">4</a> b). The initial CE decreases with an increase of the GO ratio in the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid (Figure S5 in the Supporting Information), which may be ascribed to the irreversible reduction of GO. Besides, the incorporation of GO induces a slight increase of the SSA, which may also lead to more irreversible reactions for the formation of a SEI.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="chem201703418-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6f8bed57-689f-45e4-b942-a0de80e345b3/chem201703418-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6f8bed57-689f-45e4-b942-a0de80e345b3/chem201703418-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ce0f4f00-b17c-4e07-bcbe-8869595d6194/chem201703418-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6f8bed57-689f-45e4-b942-a0de80e345b3/chem201703418-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201703418-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201703418" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of a) Na<sub>2</sub>TP, b) the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid, and c) mixed 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP (i.e., physically mixed GO (5 wt %) and Na<sub>2</sub>TP) at 30 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (0.1 C) in the voltage range 0.001–2.5 V. d) Cyclic performance of the three samples for 100 cycles at 0.1 C. e) Rate performance of the three samples.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Moreover, a much better cyclic performance of the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid than for Na<sub>2</sub>TP is observed. A reversible capacity of 235 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> is reserved for the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid after 100 cycles, indicating a capacity retention of 66.6 % whereas Na<sub>2</sub>TP only reserves a capacity of 181 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles with a capacity retention of 49.3 %. As indicated by the CV curves of the electrodes after 100 cycles in Figures <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0005">5</a> a and b, the featured peaks of both Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid are weakened due to the pulverization of Na<sub>2</sub>TP, although 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP still exhibits a larger peak current correlating with its higher reversible capacity. The SEM images of Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid after 50 cycles are shown in Figure S7 in the Supporting Information, from which a more stable structure for the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid can be clearly observed. This further suggests the improved structure stability by the GO wrapping. In the anti-solvent precipitated GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid, Na<sub>2</sub>TP particles are wrapped by GO through π–π stacking and this structure can be maintained after the slurry coating process for the electrode preparation (Figure S6 in the Supporting Information). GO can enhance the cycle stability because of the following two reasons. First, GO intensifies the electrical conductivity of the Na<sub>2</sub>TP particles after being electrochemically reduced. Second, the flexible structure of GO helps to buffer the volume change of the Na<sub>2</sub>TP particles during the charge/discharge process. The 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid has the highest capacity retention among all GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrids with different GO contents (Figure S5 in the Supporting Information) because the Na<sub>2</sub>TP particles are uniformly wrapped by GO in the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid. The most of Na<sub>2</sub>TP particles are uncovered with a low GO ratio (1 wt %) whereas many aggregated GO nanosheets appeared with a high GO ratio (10 wt %). The partial wrapping cannot well maintain the structure of Na<sub>2</sub>TP during cycling, which leads to a pulverization of the particles and thus, an unsatisfied electrochemical performance. In contrast, a high GO content leads to more side reactions because of the decomposing of the functional groups. Moreover, the aggregation of GO hinders the ion diffusion in the electrochemical reactions. Thus, too low or too high GO contents do not profit to the electrochemical performance improvement of Na<sub>2</sub>TP. Such graphene or graphene oxide wrapping structures can also be used to improve the electrochemical performance of other organic anodes.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0033_R_d7907172e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Power Sources">33</a>, <a href="#chem201703418-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0035_R_d7907172e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Small">35</a></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="chem201703418-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4f681b4e-09a2-425a-a89b-0c58263abaa6/chem201703418-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4f681b4e-09a2-425a-a89b-0c58263abaa6/chem201703418-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f632e042-187a-4c8e-9415-a9a4366beffe/chem201703418-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4f681b4e-09a2-425a-a89b-0c58263abaa6/chem201703418-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201703418-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201703418" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>CV curves of a) Na<sub>2</sub>TP and b) the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid after 100 cycles with a scan rate of 2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. EIS measurements of Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid at an open-circuit potential c) before and d) after 100 cycles in the frequency range 10 mHz–100 kHz.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>For comparison, the physically mixed GO (5 wt %) and Na<sub>2</sub>TP (denoted mixed 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP) was investigated (Figure S6 d in the Supporting Information). The initial CE and the reversible capacity of mixed 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP are 23.3 % and 329 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0004">4</a> c). The irreversible capacity of the first cycle is as high as 608 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, which mainly resulted from the aggregation of GO, thereby inducing much more irreversible reactions (Figure S6 d in the Supporting Information). Because GO is not wrapped around the Na<sub>2</sub>TP surface, an enhancement of GO on the cyclic performance is not observed (Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0004">4</a> d). The mixed 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP delivers a capacity of 167 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, which is similar to Na<sub>2</sub>TP.</p>
         
         <p>In addition, the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid exhibits a better rate performance at current rates lower than 10 C. For example, the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid delivers a reversible capacity of 185 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C, whereas only 109 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> were observed for Na<sub>2</sub>TP, which further reveals the importance of the GO wrapping structure. The rate performance of the mixed 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP was similar to that of Na<sub>2</sub>TP (Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0004">4</a> e).</p>
         
         <p>In order to further demonstrate the influences of the GO incorporation on the electrochemical performance, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were performed (Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0005">5</a> c). The Nyquist plots of Na<sub>2</sub>TP and the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid contain a semicircle in the high-frequency region and an inclined line in the low-frequency region (Figures <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0005">5</a> c and S8 in the Supporting Information), which correlate with the charge-transfer impedance on the electrode/electrolyte interface and the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, respectively. It is shown that the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid has a larger charge-transfer impedance than Na<sub>2</sub>TP before cycling, which may be due to the poor conductivity of GO. After 100 cycles, the 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid has an apparently smaller charge-transfer impedance due to the reduction of GO during the first several cycles. In contrast, the mixed 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP exhibits a much higher charge-transfer impedance due to the non-uniform hybridation of GO with Na<sub>2</sub>TP (Figure S8 a in the Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>The performance of the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid as anode for SIBs is compared with the state of the art organic anodes (Figure <a href="#chem201703418-fig-0006">6</a> and Table S1 in the Supporting Information). The 5 % GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid exhibits a capacity of 235 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, which is the best among these organic anodes. Moreover, the redox potential of the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid, which is approximately 0.4 V, is the lowest except for that of NH<sub>2</sub>–Na<sub>2</sub>TP (≈0.35 V), thus making the GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid a very promising anode for SIBs. Only a few studies focused on the preparation of graphene or GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrids, and the related information are summarized in Table S2 in the Supporting Information.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0036_R_d7907172e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Bull. Korean Chem. Soc.">36</a>, <a href="#chem201703418-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0041_R_d7907172e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - RSC Adv.">41</a></span> It is shown that the anti-solvent precipitation method in this work is more feasible and scalable. More importantly, with a very low GO content, the performance of Na<sub>2</sub>TP is greatly improved.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="chem201703418-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ddeeb5d0-8dbd-402c-ba6d-51360a16523b/chem201703418-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ddeeb5d0-8dbd-402c-ba6d-51360a16523b/chem201703418-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/00fad9eb-28ba-4a36-8526-04435908bd61/chem201703418-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ddeeb5d0-8dbd-402c-ba6d-51360a16523b/chem201703418-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201703418-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201703418" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Comparison of the performance of the state of the art organic anodes for SIBs: (COONa)<sub>2</sub>Na<sub>2</sub>TP,<span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0017" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0017_R_d7907172e1023" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Environ.       <section class="article-section__content" id="chem201703418-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="chem201703418-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>GO was combined with Na<sub>2</sub>TP through a one-step anti-solvent precipitation process. GO not only stabilizes the structure of Na<sub>2</sub>TP but also enhances the electrical conductivity after being electrochemically reduced during charge/discharge. The Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid material with only 5 wt % GO shows a much better electrochemical performance with a reversible capacity of 235 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at a current rate of 0.1 C, which is the best cyclic performance among the state of the art organic anodes for SIBs. Together with its appropriate redox potential and the low costs, the electrochemically reduced GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid is a promising anode for environmentally friendly SIBs. Meanwhile, the one-step anti-solvent precipitation method proposed in this work is easy scalable and can be applied to the preparation of various high-performance organic electrodes for practical SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="chem201703418-sec-0004">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="chem201703418-sec-0004-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="chem201703418-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="chem201703418-sec-0005-title"> Synthesis of Na<sub>2</sub>TP</h3>
            
            <p>Na<sub>2</sub>TP was synthesized through the reaction of terephthalic acid (TPA) with NaOH.<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0016_R_d7907172e1116" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></span> TPA was added and dissolved in a solution of NaOH under stirring. The solution was then dropwise added to excess of ethanol under stirring to form a white precipitate, which was collected by vacuum filtration and was thrice washed with ethanol. The obtained Na<sub>2</sub>TP was then vacuum dried at 60 °C overnight.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="chem201703418-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="chem201703418-sec-0006-title"> Synthesis of GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP</h3>
            
            <p>The GO/Na<sub>2</sub>TP hybrid was prepared through an anti-solvent precipitation process. Na<sub>2</sub>TP was dissolved with deionized water and then mixed with a suspension of GO under stirring. The prepared solution was dropwise added to ethanol forming a gray precipitate immediately. The precipitate was collected by vacuum filtration and was vacuum dried at 60 °C overnight.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="chem201703418-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="chem201703418-sec-0007-title"> Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The morphology of the samples was analyzed by using a ZEISS SUPRA 55 field emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM). Phase identification of the samples was carried out by a Bruker D8 Advance X-ray diffraction (XRD) by using Cu<sub>Kα</sub> radiation (<i>λ</i>=0.154 nm) in the scan range 2 <i>θ</i>=10–60°. Fourier transform infrared spectra and Raman spectra were collected from <img alt="urn:x-wiley:09476539:media:chem201703418:chem201703418-math-0004" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/8eab4d1a-b2c0-4bca-92ac-3cfae10965c6/chem201703418-math-0004.png">
=2000 to 400 cm<sup>−1</sup> by using a Thermo Scientific Nicolet iS 50 spectrometer and from <img alt="urn:x-wiley:09476539:media:chem201703418:chem201703418-math-0005" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/db9dd4fd-165f-437c-9555-56fa9c4cd802/chem201703418-math-0005.png">
=1800 to 800 cm<sup>−1</sup> by using an HORIBA LabRAM HR800 spectrometer with <i>λ</i>=532 nm incident radiation. Nitrogen sorption measurements were accomplished at 77 K (after degassing the samples at 200 °C under vacuum for 8 h) by using a BELSORP-mini II analyzer. The specific surface area was calculated by using the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method<span><a href="#chem201703418-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201703418-bib-0042_R_d7907172e1161" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">42</a></span> according to the adsorption isotherms. Thermogravimetry analysis (TGA) and differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) analysis were carried out with a Mettler DSC3 analyzer in the temperature range 25–800 °C with a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>−1</sup> under a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="chem201703418-sec-0008">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="chem201703418-sec-0008-title"> Electrochemical measurements</h3>
            
            <p>CR2032-type coin cells were assembled to evaluate the electrochemical properties of the fabricated electrode materials in an argon-filled glovebox. The working electrode was prepared by mixing the active material, Super P, and poly(vinyl difluoride) (PVDF) binder at a weight ratio of 50:40:10 in <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidinone (NMP) and coating the obtained mixture onto a Cu foil. The weight ratio of the active material, Super P, and PVDF was 70:20:10 for the GO working electrode. The working electrodes were then vacuum dried at 110 °C overnight. The electrolyte was a solution of 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) (volume ration 1:1). Pure Na foil was used as the counter electrode and a porous glass fiber was used as the separator. The galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were performed by using a Land 2001A cell test system at room temperature. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were carried out by using a VMP3 electrochemical workstation. The CV tests were conducted in a voltage range of 0.001–2.5 V with a scan rate of 2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The EIS tests were conducted in a frequency range of 10 mHz–100 kHz at an open-circuit potential and the modulating amplitude was 10 mV.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
